GitHub Copilot/How-tos/Use Copilot agents/Use Copilot CLI Using GitHub Copilot CLI Learn how to use GitHub Copilot from the command line.

Who can use this feature? GitHub Copilot CLI is available with the GitHub Copilot Pro, GitHub Copilot Pro+, GitHub Copilot Business and GitHub Copilot Enterprise plans. If you receive Copilot from an organization, the Copilot CLI policy must be enabled in the organization's settings.

In this article The command-line interface (CLI) for GitHub Copilot allows you to use Copilot directly from your terminal. For more information, see About GitHub Copilot CLI.

Note

GitHub Copilot CLI is in public preview with data protection and subject to change.

Prerequisite Install Copilot CLI. See Installing GitHub Copilot CLI.

Using Copilot CLI In your terminal, navigate to a folder that contains code you want to work with.

Enter copilot to start Copilot CLI.

Copilot will ask you to confirm that you trust the files in this folder.

Important

During this GitHub Copilot CLI session, Copilot may attempt to read, modify, and execute files in and below this folder. You should only proceed if you trust the files in this location. For more information about trusted directories, see About GitHub Copilot CLI.

Choose one of the options:

Yes, proceed:
Copilot can work with the files in this location for this session only.

Yes, and remember this folder for future sessions:
You trust the files in this folder for this and future sessions. You won't be asked again when you start Copilot CLI from this folder. Only choose this option if you are sure that it will always be safe for Copilot to work with files in this location.

No, exit (Esc):
End your Copilot CLI session.

If you are not currently logged in to GitHub, you'll be prompted to use the /login slash command. Enter this command and follow the on-screen instructions to authenticate.

Enter a prompt in the CLI.

This can be a simple chat question, or a request for Copilot to perform a specific task, such as fixing a bug, adding a feature to an existing application, or creating a new application.

For some examples of prompts, see About GitHub Copilot CLI.

When Copilot wants to use a tool that could modify or execute files—for example, touch, chmod, node, or sed—it will ask you to approve the use of the tool.

Choose one of the options:

Yes:
Allow Copilot to use this tool. The next time Copilot wants to use this tool, it will ask you to approve it again.

Yes, and approve TOOL for the rest of the running session:
Allow Copilot to use this tool—with any options—without asking again, for the rest of the currently running session. You will have to approve the command again in future sessions.

Choosing this option is useful for a many tools—such as chmod—as it avoids you having to approve similar commands repeatedly in the same session. However, you should be aware of the security implications of this option. Choosing this option for the command rm, for example, would allow Copilot to delete any file in or below the current folder without asking for your approval.

No, and tell Copilot what to do differently (Esc):
Copilot will not run the command. Instead, it ends the current operation and awaits your next prompt. You can tell Copilot to continue the task but using a different approach.

For example, if you ask Copilot to create a bash script but you do not want to use the script Copilot suggests, you can stop the current operation and enter a new prompt, such as: Continue the previous task but include usage instructions in the script.

Tips Optimize your experience with Copilot CLI with the following tips.

Stop a currently running operation If you enter a prompt and then decide you want to stop Copilot from completing the task while it is still "Thinking," press Esc.

Include a specific file in your prompt To add a specific file to your prompt, use @ followed by the relative path to the file. For example: Explain @config/ci/ci-required-checks.yml or Fix the bug in @src/app.js. This adds the contents of the file to your prompt as context for Copilot.

When you start typing a file path, the matching paths are displayed below the prompt box. Use the arrow keys to select a path and press Tab to complete the path in your prompt.

Work with files in a different location To complete a task, Copilot may need to work with files that are outside the current working directory. If a prompt you have entered in an interactive session requires Copilot to modify a file outside the current location, it will ask you to approve access to the file's directory.

You can also add a trusted directory manually at any time by using the slash command:

/add-dir /path/to/directory If all of the files you want to work with are in a different location, you can switch the current working directory without starting a new Copilot CLI session by using the slash command:

/cwd /path/to/directory Run shell commands You can prepend your input with ! to directly run shell commands, without making a call to the model.

!git clone https://github.com/github/copilot-cli Delegate tasks to Copilot coding agent The delegate command lets you push your current session to Copilot coding agent on GitHub. This lets you hand off work while preserving all the context Copilot needs to complete your task.

You can delegate a task using the slash command, followed by a prompt:

/delegate complete the API integration tests and fix any failing edge cases Copilot will ask to commit any of your unstaged changes as a checkpoint in a new branch it creates. Copilot coding agent will open a draft pull request, make changes in the background, and request a review from you.

Copilot will provide a link to the pull request and agent session on GitHub once the session begins.

Resume an interactive session You can return to a previous interactive session, and continue your conversation with Copilot, by using the --resume command line option, then choosing the session you want to resume from the list that's displayed. You can quickly resume the most recently closed session by using the --continue command line option.

Use custom instructions You can enhance Copilot’s performance, by adding custom instructions to the repository you are working in. Custom instructions are natural language descriptions saved in Markdown files in the repository. They are automatically included in prompts you enter while working in that repository. This helps Copilot to better understand the context of your project and how to respond to your prompts.

Copilot CLI supports:

Repository-wide instructions in the .github/copilot-instructions.md file. Path-specific instructions files: .github/copilot-instructions/**/*.instructions.md. Agent files such as AGENTS.md. For more information, see Adding repository custom instructions for GitHub Copilot.

Use custom agents Custom agents are specialized versions of Copilot coding agent that you can tailor to your unique workflows, coding conventions, and use cases. Custom agents are defined using Markdown files, called agent profiles, that specify prompts, tools, and MCP servers.

Copilot CLI supports loading custom agents from the following locations:

Type Location Scope User-level custom agent local ~/.copilot/agents directory All projects Repository-level custom agent .github/agents directory in your local and remote repositories Current project Organization and Enterprise-level custom agent /agents directory in the .github-private repository in an organization or enterprise All projects under your organization and enterprise account In the case of naming conflicts, a system-level agent overrides a repository-level agent, and the repository-level agent would override an organization-level agent.

Custom agents can be used in three ways:

Using the slash command in interactive mode to select from the list of available custom agents:

/agent Calling out to custom agent directly in a prompt:

Use the refactoring agent to refactor this code block Copilot will automatically infer the agent you want to use.

Specifying the custom agent you want to use with the command-line option. For example:

copilot --agent=refactor-agent --prompt "Refactor this code block" For more information, see Creating custom agents.

Use skills You can create skills to enhance the ability of Copilot to perform specialized tasks with instructions, scripts, and resources.

For more information, see About Agent Skills.

Add an MCP server Copilot CLI comes with the GitHub MCP server already configured. This MCP server allows you to interact with resources on GitHub.com—for example, allowing you to merge pull requests from the CLI.

To extend the functionality available to you in Copilot CLI, you can add more MCP servers:

Use the following slash command:

/mcp add Fill in the details for the MCP server you want to add, using the Tab key to move between fields.

Press Ctrl+S to save the details.

Details of your configured MCP servers are stored in the mcp-config.json file, which is located, by default, in the ~/.copilot directory. This location can be changed by setting the XDG_CONFIG_HOME environment variable. For information about the JSON structure of a server definition, see Extending GitHub Copilot coding agent with the Model Context Protocol (MCP).

View context and usage statistics for the current session You can use the /usage slash command to view how many premium requests you've used in the current session, the duration of the session, how many lines of code have been edited, and the breakdown of token usage per model. When you have less than 20% of a model's token limit remaining, Copilot CLI will display a warning that the context will be truncated when the token limit is reached.

Find out more For a complete list of the command line options and slash commands that you can use with Copilot CLI, do one of the following:

Enter ? in the prompt box in an interactive session. Enter copilot help in your terminal. For additional information use one of the following commands in your terminal:

Configuration settings:

copilot help config

You can adjust the configuration settings by editing the config.json file, which is located, by default, in the ~/.copilot directory. This location can be changed by setting the XDG_CONFIG_HOME environment variable.

Environment variables that affect Copilot CLI:

copilot help environment

Available logging levels:

copilot help logging

Permissions for allowing or denying tool use:

copilot help permissions

Feedback If you have any feedback about GitHub Copilot CLI, please let us know by using the /feedback slash command in an interactive session and choosing one of the options. You can complete a private feedback survey, submit a bug report, or suggest a new feature.

Help and support Did you find what you needed?

How does GitHub Copilot CLI compare to the IDE integrations in terms of workflow efficiency, use cases, and developer experience? What are the specific advantages and trade-offs of each approach?

Office Agent
Office Agent
GitHub Copilot CLI vs IDE Integrations: Comprehensive Comparison
Workflow Efficiency Analysis
CLI Advantages
Terminal-Native Workflow: Seamless integration with existing command-line tools and scripts
Cross-Editor Compatibility: Works with any text editor (vim, nano, emacs, VS Code, etc.)
Batch Operations: Can process multiple files and perform complex multi-step operations
Session Continuity: Resume conversations and maintain context across different work sessions
Shell Integration: Direct execution of system commands with ! prefix
IDE Integration Advantages
Real-Time Assistance: Inline suggestions as you type, immediate feedback
Visual Context: Syntax highlighting, error indicators, and visual cues enhance understanding
Integrated Debugging: Copilot suggestions work within debugging workflows
UI/UX Polish: Smooth animations, hover previews, and intuitive interfaces
Zero Context Switching: No need to leave the editor environment
Use Case Specialization
CLI Excels At:
# Project-wide refactoring
copilot "Refactor all JavaScript files to use modern ES6+ syntax"

# Complex file operations
copilot "Analyze all log files and create a summary report"

# Infrastructure tasks
copilot "Set up CI/CD pipeline with proper error handling"

# Multi-language projects
copilot "Convert this Python script to equivalent Node.js code"
IDE Integrations Excel At:
Code Completion: Real-time autocomplete and suggestion refinement
Function Documentation: Instant docstring and comment generation
Bug Fixing: Contextual error resolution with immediate preview
Code Review: Inline suggestions during pull request reviews
Learning: Educational tooltips and explanations for unfamiliar code
Developer Experience Trade-offs
CLI Strengths
Aspect	Advantage
Flexibility	Works with any development environment
Power User Efficiency	Keyboard-driven, scriptable, automatable
Context Breadth	Can analyze entire project structures
Task Delegation	Hand off complex tasks to coding agents
Custom Instructions	Repository-wide behavioral customization
CLI Limitations
Aspect	Trade-off
Learning Curve	Requires command-line comfort
Visual Feedback	Text-only interface, no syntax highlighting
Interruption	Context switching from editor to terminal
Real-time Flow	Not integrated into typing workflow
Preview Limitations	Can't see changes before applying
IDE Integration Strengths
Aspect	Advantage
Seamless Flow	No context switching, stays in editor
Visual Clarity	Syntax highlighting, diff views, previews
Immediate Feedback	Real-time suggestions and corrections
Accessibility	Lower barrier to entry for new users
Rich UI	Tooltips, panels, and interactive elements
IDE Integration Limitations
Aspect	Trade-off
Editor Lock-in	Tied to specific IDE/editor
Scope Constraints	Limited to current file/project view
Complex Operations	Harder to perform multi-step, cross-file tasks
Customization	Less flexible than CLI scripting
Resource Usage	Can slow down editor performance
Optimal Usage Scenarios
Choose CLI When:
Architecture Planning: "Design a microservices architecture for this e-commerce platform"
Code Migration: "Convert this legacy PHP codebase to modern Laravel"
DevOps Tasks: "Set up monitoring and alerting for this Kubernetes cluster"
Documentation: "Generate comprehensive API documentation from these endpoints"
Analysis: "Audit this codebase for security vulnerabilities and performance issues"
Choose IDE Integration When:
Active Development: Writing new features with real-time assistance
Debugging Sessions: Fixing bugs with immediate context
Code Reviews: Reviewing pull requests with inline suggestions
Learning: Exploring unfamiliar codebases with explanatory tooltips
Rapid Prototyping: Quick iteration with immediate feedback
Hybrid Workflow Recommendations
Complementary Usage Pattern
graph LR
    A[Planning Phase] --> B[CLI: Architecture Design]
    B --> C[Development Phase] --> D[IDE: Real-time Coding]
    D --> E[Review Phase] --> F[CLI: Cross-file Analysis]
    F --> G[Deployment Phase] --> H[CLI: Infrastructure Setup]
Best Practices for Combined Usage
Start with CLI for project setup and architectural decisions
Switch to IDE for active development and debugging
Return to CLI for complex refactoring and analysis
Use CLI for deployment and infrastructure tasks
Leverage IDE for code reviews and collaborative work
Performance Considerations
CLI Performance Profile
Startup Time: ~2-3 seconds for session initialization
Memory Usage: Minimal impact on system resources
Processing Speed: Excellent for batch operations
Context Limits: Better handling of large codebases
IDE Integration Performance Profile
Responsiveness: Near-instantaneous suggestions
Memory Usage: Can impact editor performance
Processing Speed: Optimized for single-file operations
Context Limits: Limited by editor's memory constraints
Strategic Recommendations
For Individual Developers
Beginners: Start with IDE integrations for gentler learning curve
Power Users: Adopt CLI for maximum flexibility and automation
Full-Stack Developers: Use both - CLI for infrastructure, IDE for application code
For Teams
Standardization: CLI provides consistent experience across different editors
Onboarding: IDE integrations reduce friction for new team members
Complex Projects: CLI excels at project-wide operations and maintenance
For Organizations
Cost Efficiency: CLI works with any editor, reducing licensing complexity
Scalability: CLI can be integrated into CI/CD pipelines and automation
Flexibility: Supports diverse development environments and preferences
Future Considerations
The CLI represents GitHub's vision for more autonomous, task-oriented AI assistance, while IDE integrations focus on enhancing traditional development workflows. The optimal approach likely involves using both tools strategically based on the specific task at hand and developer preferences.

Alternative AI Integration Approaches
1. OpenAI API Integration
// Similar capabilities using OpenAI's Codex/GPT models
import OpenAI from 'openai';

const openai = new OpenAI({
  apiKey: process.env.OPENAI_API_KEY,
});

async function generateCode(prompt, context) {
  const completion = await openai.chat.completions.create({
    model: "gpt-4",
    messages: [
      {
        role: "system", 
        content: "You are a code assistant. Generate clean, efficient code."
      },
      {
        role: "user", 
        content: `Context: ${context}\nRequest: ${prompt}`
      }
    ],
    temperature: 0.2,
  });
  
  return completion.choices[0].message.content;
}

// Using multiple AI providers for redundancy
class MultiProviderAI {
  constructor() {
    this.providers = [
      new OpenAIProvider(),
      new AnthropicProvider(),
      new CohereProvider()
    ];
  }
  
  async complete(prompt) {
    // Try providers in order of preference
    for (const provider of this.providers) {
      try {
        return await provider.complete(prompt);
      } catch (error) {
        console.log(`Provider failed: ${provider.name}`);
      }
    }
  }
}

Custom Implementation Strategy
Phase 1: Multi-Provider Architecture
// Unified AI service with fallback providers
class AICodeService {
  constructor() {
    this.providers = [
      {
        name: 'openai',
        client: new OpenAIProvider(),
        cost: 0.06, // per 1K tokens
        priority: 1
      },
      {
        name: 'anthropic',
        client: new AnthropicProvider(),
        cost: 0.015, // per 1K tokens
        priority: 2
      },
      {
        name: 'google',
        client: new GoogleProvider(),
        cost: 0.0015, // per 1K tokens
        priority: 3
      },
      {
        name: 'local',
        client: new LocalProvider(),
        cost: 0, // hardware only
        priority: 4
      }
    ];
  }

  async generateCode(prompt, options = {}) {
    const { 
      maxCost = 0.10, 
      requirePrivacy = false,
      preferredProvider = null 
    } = options;

    // Filter providers based on requirements
    let availableProviders = this.providers.filter(p => {
      if (requirePrivacy && p.name !== 'local') return false;
      if (p.cost > maxCost) return false;
      return true;
    });

    // Try preferred provider first
    if (preferredProvider) {
      const preferred = availableProviders.find(p => p.name === preferredProvider);
      if (preferred) {
        availableProviders = [preferred, ...availableProviders.filter(p => p !== preferred)];
      }
    }

    // Attempt generation with fallback
    for (const provider of availableProviders) {
      try {
        const result = await provider.client.generate(prompt);
        await this.logUsage(provider.name, prompt.length, result.length);
        return result;
      } catch (error) {
        console.warn(`Provider ${provider.name} failed:`, error.message);
        continue;
      }
    }

    throw new Error('All providers failed');
  }
}
Phase 2: Intelligent Routing & Caching
class SmartAIRouter {
  constructor() {
    this.cache = new Map();
    this.usageTracker = new UsageTracker();
    this.costOptimizer = new CostOptimizer();
  }

  async routeRequest(prompt, context) {
    // Check cache first
    const cacheKey = this.generateCacheKey(prompt, context);
    if (this.cache.has(cacheKey)) {
      return this.cache.get(cacheKey);
    }

    // Analyze request complexity
    const complexity = this.analyzeComplexity(prompt);
    
    // Route based on complexity and cost
    const provider = this.selectOptimalProvider(complexity, context);
    
    const result = await provider.generate(prompt);
    
    // Cache successful results
    this.cache.set(cacheKey, result);
    
    return result;
  }

  selectOptimalProvider(complexity, context) {
    if (complexity.isSimple && !context.requiresPrivacy) {
      return this.providers.google; // Cheapest
    }
    
    if (context.requiresPrivacy) {
      return this.providers.local; // Private
    }
    
    if (complexity.isComplex) {
      return this.providers.openai; // Best quality
    }
    
    return this.providers.anthropic; // Balanced
  }
}

class LectureAI {

    this.aiService = new AICodeService();
    this.educationPrompts = new EducationPromptLibrary();

// Local AI server
class LocalAIServer {
  constructor() {
    this.models = {
      'codellama': new CodeLlamaModel(),
      'starcoder': new StarCoderModel()
    };
  }

  async generate(prompt, model = 'codellama') {
    const selectedModel = this.models[model];
    if (!selectedModel) {
      throw new Error(`Model ${model} not available`);
    }

    return await selectedModel.generate(prompt);
  }
}
Cost Optimization Strategy
Tiered Usage Model
class CostOptimizer {
  constructor() {
    this.tiers = {
      development: { budget: 50, providers: ['google', 'local'] },
      staging: { budget: 200, providers: ['anthropic', 'google'] },
      production: { budget: 1000, providers: ['openai', 'anthropic'] }
    };
  }

  selectProvider(environment, requestType) {
    const tier = this.tiers[environment];
    const currentSpend = this.getCurrentSpend(environment);
    
    if (currentSpend > tier.budget * 0.8) {
      return 'local'; // Switch to free option
    }
    
    return this.getBestProviderForRequest(requestType, tier.providers);
  }
}
Usage Monitoring
class UsageTracker {
  constructor() {
    this.metrics = new Map();
  }

  async trackUsage(provider, tokens, cost) {
    const key = `${provider}-${new Date().toISOString().slice(0, 7)}`; // Monthly
    
    if (!this.metrics.has(key)) {
      this.metrics.set(key, { tokens: 0, cost: 0, requests: 0 });
    }
    
    const current = this.metrics.get(key);
    current.tokens += tokens;
    current.cost += cost;
    current.requests += 1;
    
    // Alert if approaching limits
    if (current.cost > 800) { // 80% of $1000 budget
      await this.sendCostAlert(provider, current);
    }
  }
}

Data Privacy Protection
class PrivacyManager {
  sanitizePrompt(prompt, sensitivityLevel) {
    if (sensitivityLevel === 'high') {
      // Remove PII, API keys, etc.
      return this.removeSensitiveData(prompt);
    }
    return prompt;
  }

  routeToPrivateProvider(prompt) {
    // Always use local models for sensitive data
    return this.localProvider.generate(prompt);
  }
}
